By the time those records surfaced‚Äîafter a patient request took two weeks‚Äîshe was already deteriorating into crisis again. She survived. Others don't. This gap existed not because the records couldn't be shared technically, but because the systems managing that data lacked documented accountability standards. We are deeply concerned that loosening certification criteria will expand these blind spots rather than close them.<br><br>We understand the appeal of reducing regulatory burden on vendors. But certification standards exist because patients can't review code. They can't audit algorithms. They depend on third-party verification that the systems handling their most sensitive information meet basic safety and security thresholds. Model card disclosures‚Äîtransparent documentation of how AI systems are trained, what they're designed to do, and what they might miss‚Äîshould not be an optional competitive advantage. They should be foundational.<br><br>On the specific RFI questions: We support streamlined real-world testing requirements that use existing clinical workflows rather than creating parallel certification pathways. What we cannot support is removing transparency mandates themselves. A vendor attestation without independent verification is not a safeguard‚Äîit's a liability deferral. If we weaken disclosure requirements now, before AI adoption in clinical care is widespread, we will have written patient harm into the system. We will have a documented record of knowing better and choosing not to act.<br><br>We urge HHS to retain model card requirements while exploring whether the attestation process itself can be accelerated. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0007‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîBrian Nelson‚ôîAgilian Government Solutions‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîAgilian Government Solutions warns that weakening health IT certification standards will accelerate industry consolidation by making compliance costs manageable only for large, well-capitalized firms, while urging the agency to streamline redundan...‚ôîAgilian Government Solutions respectfully submits this comment on the proposed deregulatory actions affecting health IT certification and interoperability standards.<br><br>We work with health IT vendors across the country, mostly regional and mid-market players. Last quarter alone, we spent $340,000 bringing one client through the existing certification pathway. Within months, a national health system acquired them. That acquirer now lobbies for the very regulatory relaxation that would have made my client's compliance costs negligible during the transition. I'm watching this play out repeatedly, and it troubles me because the math is simple: larger entities can absorb certification burden as a cost of acquisition. Smaller competitors cannot. Weakening DSI and certification criteria will not unleash competition‚Äîit will accelerate consolidation by making the transition costs manageable only for well-capitalized acquirers and existing incumbents.<br><br>On the specific question of which certification criteria should be removed or revised: we'd support streamlining redundant attestation requirements and test procedures that duplicate functional validation already accomplished through real-world deployment data. But I want to be direct about the transparency provisions. The agency's reasoning‚Äîthat model card disclosures lack longitudinal outcome literature‚Äîconfuses the absence of published studies with the absence of real benefit. These requirements are less than three years old. You cannot measure five-year outcomes when the policy itself is younger than that. The absence of evidence is not evidence of absence. It's evidence of immaturity. Dismantling transparency because we don't yet have ten years of peer-reviewed data is premature and, frankly, dangerous. We need those disclosures to remain because they're the only mechanism preventing vendors from burying algorithmic bias or system limitations in fine print that no provider actually reads.<br><br>What I'm asking the agency to consider: tightening burden on testing procedures, yes. Removing transparency and accountability? That's where I have to push back. The smaller players we serve need standards to compete on merit. Once those standards erode, they lose the only leverage they have against acquisition at distressed valuations.‚ôî‚ôî2026-01-19T00:00:00Z‚ôî2026-01-19T00:00:00Z‚ôî2025-12-05T00:00:00Z‚ôî2026-02-03T00:00:00Z‚ôî1‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîTRUE‚ôî1‚ôîRetain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.‚ôîThe agency's claim that no public evidence supports transparency's positive impact conflates lack of published studies with lack of real-world benefit; transparency requirements are too recent and nascent to have generated robust outcome literature.‚ôîindustry‚ôîhigh‚ôîurgent‚ôîWyoming‚ôîVP of regulatory affairs‚ôî56‚ôîLast quarter, we spent $340,000 bringing one of our smaller health IT clients into compliance with the existing certification regime, only to watch them get acquired by a national player who could absorb those costs as a rounding error‚Äîand now that same acquirer is lobbying to weaken the very standards that made the smaller player vulnerable. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0008‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîStephanie Taylor‚ôîbeHuman Corp.‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîA health IT vendor argues that retaining structured DSI model card transparency requirements reduces overall burden by providing hospitals with standardized information for purchasing decisions, and that regulatory streamlining should instead targ...‚ôîThank you for the opportunity to comment on the proposed changes to health IT certification requirements. As a compliance officer at beHuman Corp, a health IT vendor in Ohio, I've spent the last year navigating multi-state certification demands, and I want to offer a perspective that might seem counterintuitive: retaining structured transparency requirements, specifically DSI model card disclosures, actually reduces burden rather than increasing it.<br><br>Last year our team spent three months just parsing certification requirements across state lines while trying to ship patient portal updates our clients needed. One colleague ended up on medical leave from the stress. What I learned is that removing requirements without maintaining transparency standards doesn't help smaller vendors like us‚Äîit just shifts the burden downstream to hospital procurement teams, who then ask us the same questions anyway, usually in less structured ways. We end up answering emails and scheduling calls instead of filling out one clear disclosure form.<br><br>Here's what concerns me about loosening DSI transparency without replacement: hospitals need detailed information to evaluate whether our software actually fits their specific workflows and patient populations. Right now, a procurement officer at Cleveland Clinic or Ohio Health can review our model disclosures and know exactly what they're getting. Remove that, and they either demand customized assessments (which costs us more than the form), or they default to the vendors with the biggest sales teams. That's not deregulation‚Äîthat's regulatory capture by the incumbents.<br><br>I'm not opposed to streamlining. But the right target isn't transparency. It's the redundant state-by-state certification pathways that forced us to hire a contractor just to manage paperwork. Removing certification criteria should mean one clear federal standard, not a fragmented market where every hospital requests different documentation. The DSI model cards are how we prove to buyers that we're serious about their safety and workflow requirements. They're a form of standardization that actually speeds purchasing decisions.<br><br>If you're asking which requirements should be revised, I'd say focus on eliminating duplicative state oversight and streamlining the real-world testing protocols so smaller vendors can afford the process. But keep the disclosure standards. They're not red tape. They're the structured data that lets procurement teams make informed decisions faster, which gets better software to patients quicker. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0015‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîRebecca Adams‚ôîAd Hoc LLC‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîA small health IT vendor argues that clear, upfront AI model card disclosure requirements should be implemented now rather than deferred, as regulatory ambiguity disproportionately burdens smaller firms through over-engineering and compliance cost...‚ôîRebecca Adams, Ad Hoc LLC<br><br>Re: Health Data, Technology, and Interoperability: ASTP/ONC Deregulatory Actions<br><br>Ad Hoc LLC is a small health IT vendor based in Mississippi. It's about who gets to innovate and who gets squeezed out.<br><br>I'm writing specifically about AI model card disclosure requirements. I know the impulse is to lighten the load on developers, and I understand it. But I'd argue that transparent AI governance standards should go the other direction‚Äîthey should be implemented now, upfront, before AI becomes endemic in decision support tools and other products. Here's why it matters to a firm like ours: uncertainty kills smaller companies. Ambiguity about what's acceptable drives vendors to over-engineer, to hire compliance consultants we can't afford, to delay product launches while we guess at what regulators will require. Clear, specific disclosure requirements about AI model performance, limitations, and validation methods are actually cheaper than the alternative‚Äîwhich is reactive enforcement after failures occur in clinical settings.<br><br>That's the ethical argument too. Waiting for documented patient harm in real-world DSI products before requiring transparency is not a smart deregulatory move. It's a deferral of accountability. Smaller vendors will absorb that cost in market trust and litigation. Larger vendors will survive it.<br><br>What I'd propose: establish baseline AI model card disclosure requirements now. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0016‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîJames Thompson‚ôîPolicy Center for Maternal Mental Health‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîThe Policy Center for Maternal Mental Health opposes eliminating AI model card disclosure requirements from health IT certification, arguing that transparency about algorithm training data, intended use, and validation is essential for preventing ...‚ôîRe: Health Data, Technology, and Interoperability: ASTP/ONC Deregulatory Actions To Unleash Prosperity<br><br>Policy Center for Maternal Mental Health respectfully submits this comment regarding the proposed revision to health information technology certification criteria, specifically the proposed elimination of detailed AI model card disclosure requirements from the Data Sharing Infrastructure certification criterion.<br><br>We appreciate the Administration's commitment to reducing unnecessary regulatory burden on health IT developers. The maternal mental health community understands that excessive certification requirements can slow innovation and increase costs. However, we urge the Department to retain the current requirement for comprehensive AI model card disclosures‚Äîincluding intended use specifications, target population definitions, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes‚Äîparticularly for clinical decision support systems and diagnostic tools used in high-stakes maternal and perinatal mental health settings.<br><br>My perspective on this issue is shaped by recent experience. Last year, my sister was diagnosed with postpartum depression after the birth of her second child. What struck me during her treatment was how fragmented her care became. Her OB-GYN and psychiatrist operated almost entirely in separate systems. Basic information about her medications, symptom progression, and mental health history couldn't flow between providers because they used incompatible systems. When we finally got her records transferred manually, critical context was missing. One provider didn't know she'd already tried a particular SSRI. The other wasn't aware of her obstetric history, which mattered for medication selection. She spent months in this gap, and I realized then that data interoperability isn't an abstract policy goal‚Äîit's about whether patients get coordinated, informed care.<br><br>But interoperability without transparency about the algorithms shaping that care creates a different kind of problem. If her psychiatrist had been relying on an AI tool to flag medication interactions or assess suicide risk, and that tool's training data didn't adequately represent women in the postpartum period, or its intended use assumed a different patient population, that tool could have caused harm. My sister would have had no way to know.<br><br>The maternal mental health field faces specific risks if AI model transparency is weakened. Postpartum depression and peripartum anxiety disorders are underdiagnosed and undertreated, particularly in Black and Hispanic women. If diagnostic support systems are trained primarily on data from non-Hispanic white populations, they may miss presentations that look different across racial and ethnic groups. The current model card requirement forces developers to make these gaps visible. Without it, a provider might adopt a tool without realizing it was never validated in the populations they serve.<br><br>We have reviewed the proposed deregulatory framework and understand the argument that model cards impose compliance costs. But we believe those costs are justified in healthcare contexts where AI directly influences clinical decisions affecting vulnerable populations. The requirement doesn't prevent innovation‚Äîit channels it toward safer, more accountable innovation. A developer building a legitimate tool should be willing to document what populations it works for and what it doesn't.<br><br>Three specific concerns drive our position:<br><br>First, maternal mental health has historically been deprioritized in clinical research and training. Postpartum psychiatric conditions are understudied compared to other mental health conditions. This means that AI training datasets are likely to underrepresent the nuances of perinatal mood and anxiety disorders. Without mandatory disclosure of training data characteristics, providers won't know whether a tool was built on adequate maternal mental health data or whether it's being applied to a population it was never tested in.<br><br>Second, the stakes of algorithmic error in this domain are high. Maternal suicide is a leading cause of pregnancy-related death in the United States. If a risk assessment tool misses warning signs in certain subpopulations, the consequences aren't regulatory violations‚Äîthey're preventable deaths. A model card that clearly identifies validation gaps and known limitations is a basic accountability mechanism.<br><br>Third, we've seen how information fragmentation affects maternal mental health outcomes. The solution is better interoperability, but interoperability without transparency about the algorithms moving data between systems doesn't protect patients. It just distributes flawed information faster.<br><br>We recognize that some health IT developers operate on tight margins, and compliance costs matter. We also recognize that the certification process has grown complex. But the answer isn't to eliminate transparency requirements in high-stakes clinical applications. The answer is to streamline them‚Äîperhaps by creating a tiered certification pathway where basic interoperability tools face lighter requirements, while clinical decision support tools and diagnostic aids face fuller disclosure requirements.<br><br>The maternal mental health community is committed to better data sharing. We want providers to have access to complete patient histories. We want research to accelerate. But we want these improvements built on a foundation of transparency about how algorithms work and who they've been tested on. Removing model card requirements in the name of deregulation would trade short-term compliance cost savings for long-‚ôî‚ôî2026-01-17T00:00:00Z‚ôî2026-01-17T00:00:00Z‚ôî2025-12-05T00:00:00Z‚ôî2026-02-03T00:00:00Z‚ôî2‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîTRUE‚ôî3‚ôîRetain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.‚ôîPatients and clinicians have a fundamental right to know whether a high-stakes health recommendation originates from a human expert or an algorithmic system, and what that system's limitations are.‚ôîadvocacy_group‚ôîmedium‚ôîfrustrated‚ôîMissouri‚ôîsenior policy analyst‚ôî40‚ôîLast year, my sister went through postpartum depression after delivering her second child, and I watched her struggle to get her OB-GYN and psychiatrist to share basic information about her medications and symptoms‚Äîthey were literally operating in separate silos while she was suffering. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0017‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîAshley Scott‚ôîRune Labs‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîRemoving AI model card disclosure requirements would not reduce burden but rather shift evaluation costs to health systems while eliminating a critical transparency standard that clinicians and IT leaders depend on to assess whether algorithmic to...‚ôîThree years ago, we were analyzing why a patient's allergy information couldn't move between two hospital systems in the same metropolitan area. She was admitted to a competing health system with a critical cardiac condition, and her cardiologist had no access to her documented penicillin allergy from a regional network's legacy system. The drug interaction nearly killed her. What struck me, sitting with the IRB afterward, was that this wasn't a technical problem‚Äîthe technology to share that data existed. It was a regulatory and market structure problem, and nobody had strong enough incentives to solve it.<br><br>That incident became the center of a three-year research protocol we designed to understand why these silos persist despite the obvious harm. The data we've collected makes clear that transparency requirements‚Äîspecifically, the detailed AI model card disclosures required under current DSI certification criteria‚Äîare doing something very different from what some commenters seem to believe. They're not slowing adoption. They're actually creating the trust conditions that make adoption possible in clinical settings where the stakes are genuinely high.<br><br>I want to be direct about what I'm concerned the proposed deregulatory actions might accomplish. Removing the requirement for detailed AI model card disclosures‚Äîthe ones that specify intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes‚Äîsounds like a burden reduction. In isolation, it probably is. But in the context of health IT adoption in real hospitals and regional networks, it removes one of the few transparent signals that clinicians and health system IT leaders actually use to evaluate whether an algorithm should be trusted with patient data.<br><br>We work with regional networks. I talk to their chief medical information officers, and their IT security teams. The conversation about whether to integrate a new clinical decision support tool or a patient monitoring algorithm isn't primarily about regulatory compliance. It's about whether they can understand what the tool does, what patient populations it was tested on, what its failure modes are, and whether the vendor can articulate any of that clearly. The model card requirement doesn't create that understanding, but it creates a forcing function‚Äîa minimum disclosure standard that makes it possible to have that conversation at all. Remove it, and you don't reduce burden; you increase it for the health systems trying to evaluate tools, because now they're in direct negotiation with every vendor about what information will be disclosed.<br><br>I'm also thinking about what happened to our patient. If the allergy information had been trapped not just in a legacy system but in an AI-driven clinical decision support tool that nobody understood, with no documented sense of what patient populations it was trained on or what edge cases it might miss, the outcome could have been worse. The regulatory framework we're discussing isn't abstract. It touches the actual reliability of information that moves between systems and the intelligibility of the algorithmic tools that process it.<br><br>The research we've been doing suggests something that might be counterintuitive to the deregulatory impulse: health IT vendors and health systems actually want these transparency standards. Not all of them, and not without grumbling about compliance costs. But the ones doing serious clinical work‚Äîthe ones whose products are embedded in workflows where lives depend on the data moving correctly and the algorithms working as intended‚Äîthey want certification standards that are credible. They want to be able to tell their customers that their tools meet a real standard, not just that they comply with whatever negotiated disclosure happened to occur.<br><br>What concerns me about removing the model card requirement is that it conflates two different things. One is reducing unnecessary bureaucratic burden‚Äîeliminating requirements that don't actually improve safety or interoperability. The other is reducing transparency in ways that make it harder for the clinical system to evaluate risk. Those are not the same thing, and I think the proposed deregulatory actions blur that distinction.<br><br>The model card requirement, as currently written, asks vendors to disclose: the intended use of the AI model, the target population it was trained and validated on, known risks and limitations, the characteristics of the training and validation datasets, the performance metrics and validation processes used, and the appropriate use cases and contraindications. That's not burdensome to articulate if you actually know your own model. And if you don't know those things about your own model, that's a problem that deregulation shouldn't solve by making it legal to not know them.<br><br>I keep coming back to the patient case because it's concrete. She survived. But the near-miss revealed something about how the system fails: not because of regulation, but because incentives don't push toward interoperability and transparency. Market competition alone didn't solve it. The regional network she was admitted to had no incentive to invest in integrating legacy systems from a competing network. The EHR vendor had no incentive to make their system transparent to external AI tools. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0028‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîAndrew Smith‚ôîAVIA Nexus Collaborative‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîThe commenter argues that proposed deregulation removing AI transparency requirements would shift burden from developers to clinicians rather than reducing it, and instead recommends streamlining transparency formats (such as standardized model ca...‚ôîThe work we've been doing over the last eighteen months has left me thinking carefully about what "reducing burden" actually means in health IT regulation, and I suspect I'm not alone in that.<br><br>Last spring, my team at AVIA Nexus Collaborative co-authored a grant proposal examining how fragmented EHR systems affect diagnostic accuracy in rural oncology care. When the funding came through, I realized we'd essentially built a workaround for a problem that certification standards were supposed to address. What struck me wasn't that compliance felt onerous; it was that the real burden wasn't regulatory at all. The burden was duplication. Teams across the country are solving the same interoperability puzzle independently instead of advancing treatment protocols.<br><br>That context matters for this deregulation proposal because I worry we're conflating two different problems. One is legitimate: developers spending engineering time on documentation that adds nothing. They shift it onto clinicians.<br><br>When we integrate AI-based clinical decision support into workflows, clinicians need to know what they're relying on. Not for compliance. For safety. Neither reduces burden; both increase risk.<br><br>I'd suggest that instead of removing AI transparency requirements, we streamline the format‚Äîone standardized model card template, machine-readable, reusable across certifications. They're preventing vendors from selling products without disclosure. Those are different things. A vendor who can't articulate their model's limitations probably shouldn't be in the market. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0035‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîJessica Baker‚ôîNORC at the University of Chicago‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîHealthcare AI systems require transparent documentation of their intended use, validation cohorts, and performance limitations‚Äînot as a burden on innovation, but as essential safety infrastructure that enables clinicians to deploy tools responsibl...‚ôî# Comment on Health Data, Technology, and Interoperability: ASTP/ONC Deregulatory Actions To Unleash Prosperity<br><br>The fragmentation I witnessed firsthand last year, when licensing negotiations for de-identified EHR datasets stretched to six months and cost us a talented junior researcher, isn't just an inconvenience for academic research. It's a sign of something more troubling in how we're approaching AI transparency in healthcare. I want to speak directly to the proposal to streamline Data Sharing and Interoperability (DSI) certification criteria, particularly the question of whether detailed AI model card disclosures‚Äîcovering intended use, target population, known risks, training data, and validation processes‚Äîshould be retained or relaxed.<br><br>My instinct here is to push back on the framing that transparency creates undue burden. At NORC, we work on health IT safety studies across dozens of health systems, and what we keep running into is the opposite problem: vendors claim their AI systems work, they deploy them in clinical settings, and then when we try to actually validate whether they're safe for the populations that matter most, the information just isn't there. Last spring, when we needed to understand how an EHR vendor's sepsis prediction model performed in rural hospitals‚Äîwhere case presentations can differ significantly‚Äîwe hit a wall. The vendor hadn't publicly documented their training data composition, didn't have clarity on how the model was validated across different hospital sizes, and flagged our request for this information as a competitive threat. This isn't theoretical. This directly delayed a safety analysis that could have informed whether that model was suitable for deployment in smaller health systems.<br><br>I understand the argument that certification requirements slow adoption and give incumbents an advantage by raising compliance costs. That's plausible. But I'd offer a different angle on what's actually happening. The certification criterion that requires model cards isn't preventing innovation‚Äîit's creating a floor for what "AI in healthcare" means. When a vendor can put an algorithm into production without documenting its intended use case, its validation cohort, or the populations where it's known to underperform, that's not innovation. That's noise. And clinical teams are left to guess whether the tool they're using has been tested on patients who look like theirs.<br><br>Here's what concerns me specifically about relaxing this requirement. Healthcare AI is not like consumer AI. If a recommendation engine gets your movie preference wrong, you watch something else. If a sepsis prediction model misses a deteriorating patient in a community hospital because it was only validated in academic medical centers, someone dies. The safety argument here isn't academic hand-wringing. It's the difference between systems that clinicians can actually implement with confidence and systems that get deployed and then create liability questions nobody can answer because nobody documented what the system was supposed to do in the first place.<br><br>The model card requirement, as currently framed, asks vendors to disclose: the system's intended use and clinical decision point, the target population characteristics, the datasets used for training and validation (including their size, source, and demographic composition), known performance limitations and failure modes, the metrics used to validate the system, and how performance was assessed across different patient subgroups. That's not onerous. That's baseline documentation. Any vendor serious about healthcare AI should already have this information internally. The certification requirement just makes it visible.<br><br>What I've observed in conversations with health IT developers is that some of the resistance comes from intellectual property concerns‚Äîthey worry that detailed model cards expose proprietary training approaches. That's fair, but it's also solvable. You don't need to disclose the exact architecture or training algorithm to disclose the intended use case, the validation cohort, and the known performance gaps. Roche publishes detailed information about their diagnostic tests without revealing proprietary chemistry. The FDA's approval process for medical devices requires this kind of transparency without requiring companies to hand over their source code.<br><br>The second argument I hear is that model cards create barriers to market entry for smaller vendors. Smaller companies can't afford the compliance overhead, so the requirement protects the big players. Again, there's something to that. But the solution isn't to eliminate the requirement‚Äîit's to clarify what a good-faith model card actually requires. A startup can document their training data and validation approach without the same resources a major EHR vendor needs for other compliance activities. What they can't do is deploy a clinical AI system responsibly without knowing what they built.<br><br>I keep coming back to our experience last year because it illustrates why this matters in practice. We were trying to do something that should be routine: assess whether an existing AI system in production was performing safely across different hospital types. We had IRB approval, we had data use agreements, we had all the technical infrastructure. What we lacked was basic documentation from the vendor about how‚ôî‚ôî2026-01-01T00:00:00Z‚ôî2026-01-01T00:00:00Z‚ôî2025-12-05T00:00:00Z‚ôî2026-02-03T00:00:00Z‚ôî2‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîTRUE‚ôî2‚ôîRetain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.‚ôîThe 'black box' nature of unvetted AI algorithms represents a systemic adoption barrier; model cards are one of the few existing standards that demystify AI design and build the trust necessary for responsible clinical deployment.‚ôîacademic‚ôîhigh‚ôîconcerned‚ôîWest Virginia‚ôîpostdoctoral fellow‚ôî65‚ôîLast spring, when our health IT interoperability study at NORC required us to obtain de-identified datasets from three major EHR vendors, we encountered a six-month licensing negotiation that nearly derailed our entire project timeline‚Äîand I watched a talented junior researcher accept a position elsewhere rather than wait. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0036‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîMatthew Wright‚ôîAinsley Advisory Group‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîA mid-market AI clinical decision support vendor argues that while compliance burden reduction is needed, eliminating model card disclosure requirements would sacrifice transparency for cost savings‚Äîand proposes instead streamlining redundant test...‚ôîThank you for the opportunity to comment on the proposed certification and standards revisions. We appreciate the intent to reduce unnecessary burden on developers, though I want to flag what worries me about the current direction.<br><br>Ainsley Advisory Group sits in an awkward middle. We're too small to absorb the compliance costs that larger vendors treat as table stakes, but we're big enough that we actually care whether the hospitals using our AI-based clinical decision support tools can trust them. That's where I think this proposal gets the math wrong.<br><br>Last year we nearly lost a contract with a mid-sized hospital network in Massachusetts because a smaller competitor could undercut us 30 percent on implementation. Their compliance overhead was just lower. I kept thinking there had to be a way to level that playing field without hospitals having to guess whether an AI algorithm is actually safe to deploy in their wards. That's the part that concerns me about removing AI model card disclosure requirements. Model cards aren't bureaucratic theater. They're how a clinician or informatics director actually sees what an algorithm was tested on, what its failure modes are, where it performs poorly. Remove those, and you haven't leveled the playing field for innovation‚Äîyou've just lowered the floor for transparency, and hospitals pay for that with risk they can't even quantify.<br><br>What I think gets lost in this deregulation framing is that the compliance burden doesn't exist because regulators enjoy paperwork. It exists because bad algorithmic outcomes hurt patients. The question isn't whether to eliminate model card requirements. It's whether we can design smarter certification that removes the redundant testing, the duplicative attestations, the administrative make-work, while preserving the disclosure criteria that actually prevent harm.<br><br>For instance, real-world testing and attestation requirements eat up months and dollars for vendors of our size. But not all of that testing serves a safety function. Some of it's just checking boxes. I'd support revisions that consolidate or eliminate redundant real-world testing pathways‚Äîsay, allowing evidence from one health system deployment to substitute for duplicative testing at another system with similar patient populations and workflows. That's burden reduction that doesn't gut transparency.<br><br>Same with certification criteria more broadly. There's probably room to remove criteria that don't actually correlate with patient safety outcomes, or that duplicate what other regulations already require. But the model card requirement, the interoperability standards that let hospitals actually integrate these tools into their systems safely‚Äîthose aren't the problem. The problem is that it all costs the same whether you're Cerner or a 40-person startup in Providence.<br><br>I'm not asking for special treatment for mid-market vendors. I'm asking that we don't mistake deregulation for the same thing as safety. Smaller companies should be able to compete fairly. But fair competition doesn't mean hospitals have to choose between low cost and knowing whether an algorithm is going to make mistakes at the point of care. We understand the impulse behind the broader deregulatory initiative. We get it. The compliance burden is real. It costs money, and that money could theoretically go toward actual innovation. When we implemented our latest EHR system two years ago, we contracted with a vendor who assured us their system met all relevant certification standards. What we discovered during go-live was that their API documentation didn't align with the newer interoperability requirements‚Äînot because the underlying technology was incapable, but because updating their certification would have cost six figures in compliance work. The vendor made a business judgment that the cost wasn't worth it. So we paid $40,000 to a third-party developer to bridge the gap and ensure our patients' records could actually follow them when they saw specialists in the neighboring county. That's not an unusual story anymore. It's becoming the expected route.<br><br>But here's what troubles me about applying the same "deregulation as a solution" logic to AI certification: in that EHR case, the gap was about data formatting and connectivity. If the API bridge didn't work perfectly, it meant duplicate data entry or missing information. It was inefficient and frustrating. It cost us money. But it didn't make a clinical decision worse in a direct way. An AI algorithm lacking transparent documentation about its training data, known failure modes, target population, and validation methodology operates in a different risk category. If that algorithm is making recommendations about patient diagnosis or treatment, and no one has clear documentation about what populations it was designed for and what biases it might carry, we're operating in the dark in a way that affects patient care directly.<br><br>We've been piloting a predictive tool for identifying patients at high risk of hospital readmission. The vendor provided basic documentation, but when we dug deeper‚Äîand we had to hire a consultant to do this‚Äîwe discovered the tool was trained primarily on data from urban academic medical centers. Our patient population is about 40 percent rural, with different comorbidity patterns and different healthcare access patterns. The algorithm's performance degraded noticeably when we applied it to our rural patient population. Our clinical staff rightfully wanted to understand why before they relied on the tool in their workflow. If we had only the basic vendor information without detailed model cards, we would have had no way to identify this limitation ourselves.<br><br>The DSI criterion requiring detailed AI model cards is not bureaucratic overhead. It's the documentation that lets a healthcare organization like ours actually evaluate whether a tool is appropriate for our patient population. It lets us understand what the algorithm was trained to do, not just what the vendor claims it will do. It enables our clinicians to use AI tools with appropriate skepticism and context, rather than as black boxes they're expected to trust.<br><br>I'm sympathetic to the idea that overly prescriptive certification processes can stifle innovation. There are absolutely unnecessary compliance hoops in the current system. But the DSI model card requirement is doing work that matters. It's doing work that would be expensive and time-consuming for every single healthcare organization to replicate independently if the documentation didn't exist. Right now, if we want to evaluate an AI tool, we can ask for the model card. If that requirement goes away, we either trust the vendor's claims uncritically, or we hire consultants and data scientists to reverse-engineer the algorithm ourselves. The first option is bad for patient safety. The logic seems to be: reduce compliance burden, vendors can innovate faster, more vendors enter the market, competition improves products. That's not actually what we've observed. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0041‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîJames White‚ôîRune Labs‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîThe commenter argues against wholesale removal of model card disclosure requirements for clinical AI systems, contending that transparency and accountability structures are essential in high-stakes healthcare contexts, while proposing targeted reg...‚ôîThree years ago, my lab shelved a project that could have flagged dangerous drug interactions across hospital systems in real time. That absence of published studies doesn't mean the benefit isn't real; it means we haven't measured it carefully yet. In high-stakes healthcare contexts, accountability structures matter. Model card disclosures create those structures. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0044‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîRebecca Hall‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîThe commenter opposes removing transparency requirements for AI systems in healthcare, arguing that model cards and attestation standards are essential for clinicians and patients to understand how AI tools work, assess their safety, and maintain ...‚ôîMy mom's cardiologist's office lost her medication list for three days last month because their system crashed. How am I supposed to know?<br><br>My real concern isn't innovation‚Äîit's accountability. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0047‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîBrian Garcia‚ôîAlliance for Connected Care‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîThe Alliance for Connected Care opposes removing AI model card disclosure requirements for Data Segmentation Infrastructure certification, arguing that detailed documentation of model training data, validation processes, and performance across pat...‚ôîThe Alliance for Connected Care appreciates the opportunity to comment on the proposed deregulatory actions regarding health data, technology, and interoperability. Our organization has spent the past decade working directly with patient advocacy groups, healthcare providers, and health system leaders across Kentucky and the broader Southeast to identify and resolve barriers to meaningful health information exchange. What I want to focus on here is the proposed removal of detailed AI model card disclosure requirements for Data Segmentation Infrastructure certification.<br><br>I'll be direct: I understand the impulse behind deregulation. I really do.<br><br>But I'm also not going to pretend this is simple.<br><br>The current DSI certification criterion requires vendors to provide detailed AI model cards that disclose intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes. The proposal suggests this is unnecessarily burdensome documentation that slows down innovation. I want to explain why we believe retaining this requirement is actually foundational to patient safety and clinical adoption, even if it takes longer upfront.<br><br>Here's what troubles me about removing the model card requirement. When a healthcare system is deciding whether to implement a new AI-driven tool for patient data segmentation or algorithmic decision-making, they're making a choice that directly affects patient care. The clinicians using these tools need to know what they're actually relying on. A model card isn't bureaucratic overhead‚Äîit's the only mechanism that tells a cardiologist whether the algorithm making segmentation decisions was trained on a population that includes people like her patients, or whether it was validated on data that doesn't represent the communities she serves. Without that transparency, we're asking healthcare providers to adopt tools they fundamentally cannot evaluate.<br><br>This is where my concern gets more concrete. In 2022, we surveyed 47 healthcare organizations across Kentucky and Tennessee about their AI adoption practices. Forty-one of them told us they had no systematic way to understand what data their AI tools were trained on or how those tools performed in their specific patient populations. Twenty-eight said they'd encountered situations where an AI tool performed differently‚Äîsometimes significantly differently‚Äîacross racial or ethnic groups, and they had no documentation from the vendor explaining why. When I asked them what information would make them more confident in adopting these tools, the single most common answer was: "We need to know what the model was built on and whether it works for our patients."<br><br>That's not coming from regulatory zealots. That's coming from chief medical information officers and quality improvement directors who are trying to responsibly implement new technology.<br><br>The model card requirement exists because healthcare is different from other sectors where deregulation might work fine. If a consumer-facing tech company removes documentation requirements for an algorithm, the risk is usually contained to individual user experience. But when we're talking about health data segmentation and AI-driven clinical decision support, the risk propagates. A poorly understood algorithm affects not just one patient but potentially hundreds or thousands. And vulnerable populations bear the highest risk because their data is often underrepresented in training datasets. We've seen this pattern repeatedly: algorithms trained predominantly on majority populations perform worse for minority groups, and without model cards, providers can't even see that this is happening.<br><br>I also want to acknowledge something that I think gets lost in these discussions. Vendors have legitimate concerns about proprietary information. I'm not unsympathetic to that. But there's a difference between protecting trade secrets and disclosing basic information about model behavior and limitations. A vendor can protect the specific architecture of their neural network while still documenting what patient populations the model was validated on, what the accuracy rates are across different subgroups, and what known limitations exist. These aren't trade secrets. They're safety information.<br><br>What I've seen happen in other regulatory contexts is that when you remove documentation requirements in the name of reducing burden, you don't actually reduce burden on the healthcare system. You shift it. Instead of vendors providing model cards upfront, health systems end up conducting their own validation studies to figure out whether these tools work in their environment. We've had three separate health systems tell us they've had to hire external consultants to do post-deployment audits of AI tools because they couldn't get adequate documentation from vendors. That's not less burden. The patients we study deserve better.<br><br>Regarding real-world testing attestations: I'd urge caution before relaxing these requirements. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0052‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîKimberly White‚ôîAmerican College of Cardiology‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîA health policy educator and cardiologist argues against weakening AI model documentation requirements in the proposed interoperability rule, contending that detailed model cards‚Äîwhich disclose intended use, validation methods, and performance lim...‚ôîThe preliminary data my colleague presented last month at the University of Oklahoma College of Medicine troubled me more than I initially realized. One patient's medication list in System A didn't match System B's record; another's lab values required manual verification because the timestamps didn't align. This wasn't a edge case. It was happening routinely, affecting patient safety decisions, and no one seemed to have clear visibility into why.<br><br>I've spent the last decade teaching health policy students that interoperability is foundational to modern healthcare delivery. The statutory framework is sound. The regulatory intent is clear. But when I watched clinicians work around broken systems because the certification process had become largely performative, I realized the gap between policy aspiration and clinical reality had widened to the point where it demanded comment.<br><br>The proposed rule appears designed to reduce compliance burden on health IT developers by relaxing detailed certification criteria, particularly around artificial intelligence model documentation. I understand the competitive argument. I also understand the innovation argument. But I'm writing to push back on the specific proposal to eliminate or weaken the Data Segmentation for Interoperability (DSI) certification requirement for detailed AI model card disclosures, and I want to be direct about why.<br><br>The model card requirement‚Äîwhich mandates disclosure of a model's intended use, target population, known performance limitations, training data characteristics, validation methodologies, and risk profiles‚Äîexists because healthcare AI operates in a domain where failures cascade. When an algorithm trained primarily on data from patients aged 65 and older gets deployed to a mixed-age cardiology population, the clinical consequences aren't abstract. They're measured in diagnostic delays, missed arrhythmias, and treatment decisions made on incomplete information. The model card doesn't prevent this problem entirely, but it creates a traceable record of what the system was designed to do, who it was validated against, and what its known limitations are. Remove that requirement and you remove the accountability mechanism that allows clinicians and health systems to make informed adoption decisions.<br><br>Here's what worries me: the proposal frames detailed model documentation as a compliance burden that slows innovation. That's technically true. It does require developers to document their work more thoroughly. But the framing misses what transparency actually provides in high-stakes clinical settings. When our cardiologists are deciding whether to trust an AI-assisted diagnostic tool with patient data, they need to know if the underlying model was validated on datasets that actually represent their patient population. They need to know if the model performs differently across demographic subgroups. They need to know what the false positive and false negative rates actually are, not in a whitepaper, but in a standardized format that allows for systematic comparison across products.<br><br>I'm not arguing against innovation. I'm arguing that meaningful innovation in clinical AI requires trust, and trust requires transparency that goes beyond what marketing materials or general privacy policies can provide. The model card requirement creates a level playing field where developers compete on the quality of their validation and the honesty of their risk disclosures rather than on whose marketing narrative is most persuasive. That's not regulatory burden‚Äîthat's market infrastructure.<br><br>The other issue I keep circling back to is what happens to patient safety when we assume that competition alone will drive the transparency we need. The EHR interoperability problem I mentioned earlier isn't a failure of bad actors. It's a failure of a market where vendors have insufficient incentive to truly interoperate because lock-in is profitable. The certification requirement for DSI model cards creates a similar dynamic in reverse: it makes hiding limitations costly, which means vendors have to actually solve problems rather than obscure them. Remove the requirement, and we're betting that competitive pressure will somehow overcome the economic incentive to keep proprietary advantages private. I haven't seen evidence that works in healthcare IT historically.<br><br>The American College of Cardiology has been engaged with ONC on health IT policy for years, and we've generally supported moves toward greater openness and standards-based approaches. We've also supported regulatory streamlining when it genuinely reduces burden without compromising patient safety oversight. But I need to be specific about where this particular proposal misses the mark: it conflates reduced compliance overhead with increased innovation, but in healthcare AI, those aren't the same thing. The most valuable innovation is the kind that can be transparently validated, compared, and adopted with confidence. Model cards aren't obstacles to that innovation. They're infrastructure for it.<br><br>There's also a practical implementation question that I think deserves attention. If ONC removes the detailed model card requirement from DSI certification, what replaces it? Does certification become purely functional testing without visibility into the underlying system's design and‚ôî‚ôî2026-01-31T00:00:00Z‚ôî2026-01-31T00:00:00Z‚ôî2025-12-05T00:00:00Z‚ôî2026-02-03T00:00:00Z‚ôî2‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîTRUE‚ôî2‚ôîRetain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.‚ôîThe agency's claim that no public evidence supports transparency's positive impact conflates lack of published studies with lack of real-world benefit; transparency requirements are too recent and nascent to have generated robust outcome literature.‚ôîacademic‚ôîhigh‚ôîurgent‚ôîOklahoma‚ôîprofessor of health policy‚ôî31‚ôîWhen my colleague at OU presented preliminary findings last month showing that three competing EHR vendors had each built incompatible data-export protocols‚Äîforcing cardiologists to manually reconcile patient records across systems‚ÄîI realized we were watching market consolidation actively harm the very interoperability standards I've been teaching about for a decade.‚ôîRetain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîTrue‚ôî‚ôî808
CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0053‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîLisa Scott‚ôîThe Society of Thoracic Surgeons‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîThe Society of Thoracic Surgeons opposes relaxing AI model card disclosure requirements in health data certification standards, arguing that detailed transparency about model training data, intended use, target populations, and known failure modes...‚ôîThe Society of Thoracic Surgeons appreciates the opportunity to comment on the proposed changes to health data certification and interoperability standards. But I keep coming back to what happened with that patient, and I think it points to what we need to preserve in this conversation.<br><br>The proposed rule would relax the requirement for detailed AI model card disclosures from developers. Specifically, it would reduce the DSI certification criterion that currently mandates transparency about intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes. I understand the impulse‚Äîcompliance is expensive, and it slows product launches. I get it. But in cardiothoracic surgery, when we're adopting AI tools to read imaging or assess risk, we need to know what we're actually getting. We need to know what population the model was trained on, because what works well for 50-year-old men from Boston doesn't necessarily work the same way for a 67-year-old woman with multiple comorbidities. We need to know the known failure modes, not because we want to be bureaucratic, but because we need to make informed decisions about when to trust the tool and when to double-check.<br><br>Here's what worries me about loosening this standard: we've seen in other industries what happens when companies can say "our algorithm works great" without showing the actual data behind that claim. In healthcare, that's not just a competitive problem‚Äîit's a patient safety problem. And it's also a problem for hospitals trying to adopt these tools responsibly. Right now, our member hospitals are asking vendor questions that should be answerable: What data was this model trained on? What's the performance across different subgroups? What are the documented failure modes? When vendors can't answer these questions because they're not required to keep or share that information, hospitals either have to do expensive validation studies on their own, or they adopt tools without really understanding them.<br><br>The certification criterion we're talking about isn't bureaucratic overhead. It's documentation. The model card requirement exists because surgeons and hospital IT teams need to know what they're implementing. When a vendor tells me their risk stratification model is "highly accurate," I need to see the actual validation data, the populations it was tested on, whether it performs equally well across racial and ethnic groups. That's not asking for a novel safeguard‚Äîthat's asking for transparency about something that already exists, that the company already has internally, but often doesn't share.<br><br>What I keep thinking about is this: we can reduce burden on developers without removing the requirement to disclose what their models do. A streamlined model card format would be better than no requirement at all. A standardized template that's the same across vendors‚Äîthat actually reduces burden because developers don't have to figure out what each hospital system wants to know. It's not a trade-off between innovation and safety; it's about getting transparency into a format that works for everyone.<br><br>I also want to be honest about something else. The fragmentation issue is real. Our surgeons do work across multiple health systems, and the inability to move imaging data and clinical records seamlessly is a genuine problem that slows down care. We support better interoperability. We support standards that let data move more freely. But interoperability and transparency aren't opposing values. You can have both. You can have better data exchange *and* require vendors to disclose how their AI systems work.<br><br>One more thing: I've heard from our member hospitals that they're concerned about liability if they adopt an AI tool that turns out to have a major limitation that wasn't disclosed. If we remove the requirement for vendors to document what their models do and don't do well, we're shifting that risk entirely onto the health systems that use them. That's not innovation-friendly‚Äîthat's risk-shifting. Vendors have an incentive to downplay limitations if they're not required to document them. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0063‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîMelissa Perez‚ôîCoalition for Health AI (CHAI)‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîThe Coalition for Health AI supports deregulation of AI certification requirements but opposes removing transparency standards like model cards, which provide health systems essential information about AI tool validation, performance limitations, ...‚ôîRe: Request for Information on Health Technology Ecosystem Deregulatory Actions<br><br>The Coalition for Health AI appreciates the opportunity to respond to this RFI. It was inefficient and worrying‚Äîthe kind of fragmented care that can delay diagnosis and burden patients. They're decision-making data. When a hospital procurement team evaluates an AI tool for patient scheduling, sepsis prediction, or imaging analysis, they need to know whether the model was validated on their patient population, what happens when it encounters edge cases, and how it interacts with their existing workflows. A rural hospital serving a population with different comorbidity patterns than the validation cohort is making a different risk calculation than an academic medical center. Model cards let them make that calculation informed.<br><br>Removing transparency requirements doesn't reduce burden; it shifts burden onto health systems. It forces clinical leaders to either adopt tools blindly or invest their own resources reverse-engineering what vendors should have disclosed upfront. Responsible vendors with solid products have nothing to fear from transparency‚Äîit's actually a competitive advantage. The vendors cutting corners benefit from opacity.<br><br>Real world testing and attestation requirements are worth revisiting for streamlining, but not elimination. Consider a middle ground: allow vendors to satisfy validation requirements through a combination of premarket testing and real-world performance monitoring agreements, with clear timelines‚Äîsay, 90 days of deployment data rather than endless premarket trials. Health systems could commit to structured data sharing for accountability, and vendors get faster market access without sacrificing safety signals.<br><br>The stakes are patient safety and informed institutional choice. I don't want another family watching their mother's care fragment because the systems holding her data couldn't communicate. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0065‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîBrian Rodriguez‚ôîTMF Health Quality Institute‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîTMF Health Quality Institute opposes relaxing AI model card disclosure requirements in the proposed health IT deregulation, arguing that detailed transparency about clinical decision support tools' intended use, training data, validation methods, ...‚ôîRE: Health Data, Technology, and Interoperability: ASTP/ONC Deregulatory Actions To Unleash Prosperity<br><br>TMF Health Quality Institute respectfully submits this comment on the proposed deregulatory framework for health IT certification and interoperability standards.<br><br>We support the goal of reducing unnecessary burden on developers and accelerating innovation. The current certification process does create friction, and we recognize that some requirements may be outdated. But I need to be direct about what concerns me most in these proposals: the relaxation of AI model card disclosures for clinical decision support tools.<br><br>My organization focuses on quality measurement and health IT adoption across Medicare and Medicaid programs. We work with health systems, payers, and vendors to understand how tools actually function in practice. What I've learned is that when systems fail silently‚Äîwhen a clinical algorithm makes a bad prediction and nobody knows why‚Äîthe damage compounds. A cardiologist might trust a risk score that was trained on a population that doesn't match her patient. A hospital might deploy a sepsis alert without understanding what patient characteristics it was designed for. These aren't hypothetical risks. We see them in implementation failures that cost money and, more importantly, delay care or lead to wrong decisions.<br><br>The proposed rule would allow developers to submit AI model cards with significantly less detail: intended use, target population, known limitations, training data characteristics, and validation processes would no longer be required elements of certification. The stated rationale is that this reduces compliance costs and lets innovation move faster. I understand that argument. But here's what troubles me: in healthcare, faster isn't always better if it means less visibility into how a tool behaves.<br><br>Let me be concrete. Last year, I watched a mid-sized health system implement a predictive model for hospital readmission. The vendor provided minimal documentation about which populations the model was validated on. When the system went live, it flagged certain groups of patients at disproportionately high risk‚Äîbut only because the training data had skewed patterns, not because those patients actually had worse outcomes. The health system wasted resources on interventions for the wrong people while missing real risk elsewhere. They discovered this only after three months and a frustrated quality officer dug into the model's behavior. If there had been upfront transparency about training data composition and validation cohorts, this would have been caught during planning, not after implementation.<br><br>That's why we believe the DSI certification criterion requiring detailed model card disclosures should be retained. Not because we want to slow down innovation, but because transparency is how clinicians and health IT teams build appropriate trust in tools. When a cardiologist knows that an AI model was validated on a diverse patient population and was tested on outcomes that matter in her setting, she can integrate it thoughtfully. When she doesn't know these things, she either ignores the tool or over-trusts it. Neither outcome is good.<br><br>We're also aware that some vendors will argue detailed model cards are expensive to produce. That's fair. But the alternative‚Äîdeploying tools with minimal understanding of their strengths and limitations‚Äîcreates costs downstream: implementation failures, wasted interventions, clinician skepticism, and ultimately slower adoption of tools that could actually help.<br><br>There's a middle path here. The rule could maintain the model card requirement but streamline the certification process in other ways‚Äîperhaps by allowing vendors to submit cards in a standardized format, or by extending timelines for review to reduce administrative burden without cutting substance. CMS and ONC could also provide clearer templates for what "sufficient detail" looks like, which would reduce the guesswork developers face.<br><br>I also want to note that this isn't just about compliance. When a vendor has taken the time to document what a tool is designed for, what populations it was tested on, and what its actual performance was, that work makes the tool better. It forces clarity. We've seen vendors discover gaps in their own validation processes while preparing documentation‚Äîand then they fix those gaps before deployment. That's a feature, not a bug.<br><br>The broader deregulatory push makes sense in many areas. But in clinical AI, where decisions affect patient care, the guardrails around transparency matter. Opening up data pipes and reducing friction is valuable. Lowering the bar on what developers have to tell us about how their tools work is a different choice, and I think it's the wrong one.<br><br>We urge HHS to retain the detailed AI model card disclosure requirement as part of DSI certification. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0075‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîDonna Sanchez‚ôîAmerican College of Surgeons‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîThe American College of Surgeons opposes the removal of detailed AI model card disclosure requirements from health IT certification criteria, arguing that transparency about intended use, target populations, risks, and validation processes is esse...‚ôîThe American College of Surgeons thanks the Office of the National Coordinator and the Assistant Secretary for Technology Policy for this opportunity to comment on the proposed modifications to the Health Data, Technology, and Interoperability certification criteria. We have substantial concerns about the proposed removal of detailed AI model card disclosure requirements, particularly the provisions requiring documentation of intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes. Our position stems not from opposition to innovation or market competition, but from direct observation of what happens when those safeguards erode in clinical practice.<br><br>Two years ago, I retired from UC San Francisco after 28 years working in health services research and biostatistics. My last major project before leaving documented the interoperability failures across our hospital system's three competing EHR platforms. We submitted three separate grant applications to understand how vendors' refusal to share data schemas was affecting patient safety outcomes. None were funded. What remained clear to me, however, was this: when physicians can't see a complete picture of a patient's medical history across systems, they duplicate diagnostic testing, miss critical context, and occasionally make treatment decisions that could have been different if the information had been available and transparent. The problem wasn't complexity. It was opacity. Vendors had no incentive to reveal how their systems worked or what data they held.<br><br>That experience shaped how I think about the proposed rule changes, especially regarding AI model transparency in clinical decision-support tools and other high-stakes applications. The certification criteria requiring detailed model cards aren't bureaucratic overhead. They're the clinical equivalent of requiring surgeons to document their operative techniques, complication rates, and patient selection criteria. They're foundational to trust, and trust is what allows tools to be adopted in the first place.<br><br>Let me be direct about what concerns me. The proposed deregulation assumes that market competition and vendor reputation will naturally incentivize transparency and safety disclosure. In my experience, that assumption doesn't hold in healthcare. Vendors compete on speed to market and feature sets, not on transparency about model limitations. A hospital system evaluating two competing AI-based triage tools will choose based on cost, integration ease, and vendor support relationships‚Äînot because one vendor provided exhaustive documentation of the populations on which the model was trained or the failure modes that might emerge in low-resource settings. Once the tool is adopted, changing course is expensive and disruptive. By then, the opportunity to make an informed decision based on model documentation has passed.<br><br>The model card requirement serves three distinct functions that the proposed rule seems to conflate with unnecessary regulation. First, it enables clinical adoption. Surgeons and hospital administrators need to understand the bounds of a tool's applicability. If an AI model for predicting surgical site infections was trained primarily on data from large teaching hospitals, we need to know that before implementing it in a community hospital with different patient demographics, different surgical volume, and different post-operative monitoring capabilities. I've watched clinicians reject tools not because they were ineffective, but because they couldn't verify that the tool had been validated in populations like theirs. Transparency accelerates adoption. Opacity breeds appropriate skepticism.<br><br>Second, model cards create accountability. When something goes wrong‚Äîand in clinical AI, something eventually will‚Äîthere needs to be a traceable record of what the developers claimed the tool could do, what populations it was tested on, and what risks were disclosed. This isn't about litigation; it's about learning. If an AI model fails to flag a particular patient subgroup at high risk, we can't improve the system if we don't know whether that failure represents a gap in the training data, a gap in the validation process, or a genuine limitation of the modeling approach. Documentation enables the feedback loop that makes systems safer over time.<br><br>Third, and perhaps most important, model cards protect patients. A patient has a right to know when a decision affecting their care was informed by an algorithmic tool, what that tool's track record is, and what gaps in its knowledge or applicability might exist. Right now, many patients don't even know when AI is involved in their care. The certification requirement for detailed disclosure doesn't eliminate algorithmic bias or algorithmic error‚Äîthose are problems that require ongoing technical work‚Äîbut it makes those problems visible and addressable.<br><br>The College recognizes the legitimate concerns about regulatory burden on developers and the desire to accelerate innovation. We're not arguing that every health IT tool requires the same level of scrutiny. But the distinction the rule should draw is between different risk levels and use cases, not between disclosure and non-disclosure. An AI model that recommends a diagnostic imaging study operates in a different risk environment than one that optimizes surgical scheduling or manages supply chain logistics. The certification criteria should be tiered accordingly. Remove burdensome requirements for low-risk applications. Strengthen them for high-stakes clinical decisions.<br><br>What we're reading in the proposed rule,‚ôî‚ôî2025-12-30T00:00:00Z‚ôî2025-12-30T00:00:00Z‚ôî2025-12-05T00:00:00Z‚ôî2026-02-03T00:00:00Z‚ôî2‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîTRUE‚ôî2‚ôîRetain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.‚ôîThe agency's claim that no public evidence supports transparency's positive impact conflates lack of published studies with lack of real-world benefit; transparency requirements are too recent and nascent to have generated robust outcome literature.‚ôîacademic‚ôîhigh‚ôîresigned‚ôîCalifornia‚ôîbiostatistician‚ôî65‚ôîWhen I retired from my position at UC San Francisco two years ago, I watched our hospital's EHR integration project stall because three competing vendors refused to share their data schemas with each other‚Äîa problem I'd documented in three separate grant applications that never gained traction. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0083‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîDaniel Smith‚ôîAlliance for Connected Care‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîRemoving AI model card disclosure requirements from data sharing and interoperability certification would undermine clinician trust and informed adoption of healthcare tools, as hospitals and health systems need standardized transparency about how...‚ôîMy mother was in St. Luke's in Boise for five days last month with a serious infection, and I watched the ER doctor actually pick up the phone to call her cardiologist's office to request a fax of test results from 2022. In 2024. Her previous records were locked in systems that don't communicate with each other, and it took four additional hours to get her the right antibiotics because no one could access her full medical history. She was scared. I was furious. But I also understand why people worry about privacy and security in healthcare data. That said, watching someone you care about suffer because hospitals can't exchange information is a different kind of risk than a theoretical data breach.<br><br>The Alliance for Connected Care has spent the last several years working with patient advocates, providers, and health systems on exactly these questions‚Äîhow do we move data between systems safely and meaningfully? And what I've come to understand is that transparency in how AI and algorithmic tools work in healthcare is not opposed to interoperability. It's foundational to it. If we strip away the requirement that developers disclose detailed model cards‚Äîthe intended uses, target populations, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes‚Äîwe don't actually get faster innovation. We get faster adoption of tools that no one understands and that clinicians won't trust.<br><br>I want to focus specifically on the proposal to remove or modify the Data Sharing and Interoperability certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures. I know the framing is about reducing burden on developers. But the practical effect would be that hospitals and health systems implementing these tools would have no standardized way to understand what they're actually deploying. And that's a direct threat to the interoperability we're all trying to achieve.<br><br>Here's the thing that keeps bothering me about the deregulatory approach: interoperability without transparency creates a false choice. Yes, certification takes time and costs money. Yes, it creates friction in the development process. But the alternative isn't actually faster innovation‚Äîit's innovation that doesn't get adopted because clinicians don't trust it. I've talked to IT directors at three major health systems in Idaho and Oregon, and they've all said the same thing: they won't implement a clinical decision support tool or data exchange mechanism if they can't explain to their physicians how it works and what its limitations are. That's not regulatory burden. That's professional responsibility.<br><br>The model card requirement exists because healthcare isn't a market where you can iterate fast and fail cheap. When an algorithm fails in healthcare, people don't get refunded. They get harmed. My mother's case was straightforward‚Äîan infection that needed antibiotics. But what if the system failure had been in a diagnostic tool? What if she'd been triaged incorrectly because an AI system hadn't been validated on populations with her particular risk factors?<br><br>The certification criterion we're discussing isn't just about checking a box. It requires developers to document intended use, which means they actually have to think about who the tool is designed for and what it should and shouldn't do. It requires disclosure of known risks, which means there's a record of what was understood at the time of deployment. It requires information about training data and validation processes, which is the only way a hospital can make an informed decision about whether the tool is appropriate for their patient population.<br><br>I understand the argument that this is prescriptive and that markets should decide what information is necessary. But healthcare markets don't work that way. Hospitals don't have perfect information. Physicians are time-constrained. And patients have no visibility into how algorithmic tools are making decisions about their care. The certification criterion isn't preventing competition‚Äîdevelopers can still innovate and bring tools to market. They just have to be transparent about what they've built.<br><br>What I'm asking for is simple: keep the DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures. Not because we should be hostile to innovation or because developers are bad actors. They're not. But because transparency is how we actually build the trust and understanding necessary for interoperability to work in practice. My mother's problem wasn't solved by deregulation. It was solved by a doctor making a phone call and a medical records clerk finding a fax machine. That's not the future we're building toward.<br><br>If we remove the model card requirement, we're saying that interoperability is more important than understanding what's being shared and how it's being used. That's backwards. Interoperability is only valuable if the systems we're connecting are trustworthy and their limitations are known. The certification process isn't preventing that. It's ensuring it.<br><br>I know this isn't the main focus of the deregulatory proposal, and I know there are legitimate concerns about regulatory burden. But this particular criterion is worth preserving.
