CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0085‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîJames White‚ôîAVIA Nexus Collaborative‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîAVIA Nexus Collaborative opposes relaxing AI model transparency requirements in health IT certification, arguing that model cards documenting intended use, target populations, and validation processes are essential for clinician trust and safety r...‚ôî# Comment on ASTP/ONC Deregulatory Actions<br><br>AVIA Nexus Collaborative appreciates the opportunity to comment on the proposed revisions to health IT certification criteria. We work at the intersection of research and implementation‚Äîour lab develops interoperability tools for resource-constrained clinics, and we've spent the last two years trying to navigate certification requirements that were designed for vendors with compliance budgets, not academic research teams.<br><br>That said, I want to be direct about something that concerns me in the proposed deregulation framework: the language around loosening AI model transparency requirements in DSI certification. I understand the impulse to reduce burden. We felt that burden acutely when we shelved our cross-platform EHR sharing tool last year. But the specific criterion requiring detailed model cards‚Äîdocumentation of intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes‚Äîdoes something that matters in clinical contexts, and I'm not convinced removing it actually accelerates anything useful.<br><br>Here's the tension as I see it. We spent eighteen months building a tool that let three rural Colorado practices share patient data without proprietary middleware. It was technically sound. Then the licensing requirements shifted, and suddenly we needed six figures in legal review just to certify it. That's a real problem. But the solution isn't to assume that transparency requirements are the bottleneck. They're not. The bottleneck was legal risk assessment and vendor lock-in concerns that transparency, ironically, could have helped with.<br><br>I watched what happened next: a larger firm released essentially the same tool, but integrated into their ecosystem. Their model didn't have better security or accuracy. They just had the capital to absorb the compliance cost. That's not innovation. That's consolidation.<br><br>The model card requirement isn't theoretical. When we were designing our tool, we had to be explicit about which patient populations our algorithms were validated against. We used data from two of the three pilot sites and external validation on a third. We documented that our EHR deduplication logic performed differently in practices with older EHR systems versus newer ones. We identified that our data mapping assumptions broke down for certain medication classes. That work didn't slow us down‚Äîit made the tool better. And crucially, it meant the clinicians using it knew what they were getting.<br><br>The reason I'm pushing back on relaxing this particular criterion is that AI in healthcare isn't like other software. When a clinical decision support system makes a recommendation, or when an algorithm flags a patient for follow-up, clinicians need to know whether that output was validated in populations that look like theirs. They need to understand what training data was used. They need to know what the system was designed to do versus what it might accidentally do. This isn't regulatory theater. It's the difference between a tool you can trust and one you're essentially gambling with.<br><br>I'm not arguing for more burden. I'm arguing for keeping the requirement that does actual work. If the problem is that smaller organizations can't afford the legal review around compliance, then maybe the solution is streamlining the certification process itself‚Äîclearer guidance, faster review timelines, reduced fees‚Äîrather than removing the transparency requirement that actually helps clinicians and researchers like us understand what we're implementing.<br><br>What bothers me is that the deregulation proposal seems to assume that transparency requirements are what's holding back innovation. In my experience, they're not. What held us back was the cost of proving we'd complied with every possible requirement, many of which were redundant or unclear. The model card criterion was the one thing that actually made sense. It forced us to think clearly about our own limitations. And it would have made it easier to explain to clinics why they should use our tool instead of waiting for the next vendor with deeper pockets.<br><br>I understand the broader goal here: reduce unnecessary burden, let the market work, encourage competition. I'm sympathetic to that. But if we remove the one transparency requirement that actually helps clinicians understand AI systems, we're not reducing burden‚Äîwe're just redistributing it. We're making it so that only organizations large enough to absorb reputational risk from deploying poorly understood systems can compete. That's the opposite of what you're trying to achieve.<br><br>AVIA Nexus would welcome the opportunity to discuss this further or to serve as a test case for a streamlined certification pathway that preserves model transparency while reducing legal overhead. We think there's a middle path here that doesn't sacrifice the transparency that matters while still addressing the real compliance costs that slowed us down.‚ôî‚ôî2026-01-21T00:00:00Z‚ôî2026-01-21T00:00:00Z‚ôî2025-12-05T00:00:00Z‚ôî2026-02-03T00:00:00Z‚ôî2‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîTRUE‚ôî3‚ôîRetain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.‚ôîThe agency's claim that no public evidence supports transparency's positive impact conflates lack of published studies with lack of real-world benefit; transparency requirements are too recent and nascent to have generated robust outcome literature.‚ôîacademic‚ôîhigh‚ôîresigned‚ôîColorado‚ôîpostdoctoral fellow‚ôî60‚ôîMy lab spent eighteen months developing a cross-platform tool that let smaller clinics share EHR data without proprietary middleware, and we were ready to pilot it at three rural Colorado practices‚Äîuntil the licensing framework shifted and suddenly we couldn't guarantee compliance without six figures in legal review. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0087‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîJames Hall‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîWhile acknowledging the need for improved data sharing between healthcare providers, the commenter warns against reducing transparency and explainability requirements for AI systems in medical settings, arguing that patients must understand how al...‚ôîMy wife Margaret was diagnosed with diabetes two years ago, and I've been her caregiver ever since‚Äîmanaging her doctors, her medications, all of it. Right now, when a doctor tells her something, she can ask why. She can push back. But if some algorithm is making decisions in the background and nobody has to tell us how it works or what might go wrong with it, we're defenseless.<br><br>I'm not against making things easier for software companies. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0091‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîSharon Ramirez‚ôîCloudflare Inc‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîCloudflare opposes the removal of AI model card disclosure requirements from health IT certification standards, arguing that while some deregulation is appropriate, transparency about algorithmic decision-support tools is essential for patient saf...‚ôîRe: Health Data, Technology, and Interoperability: ASTP/ONC Deregulatory Actions To Unleash Prosperity<br><br>Cloudflare appreciates the opportunity to submit comments on the proposed deregulatory framework for health IT certification and interoperability standards. The current DSI criterion requires vendors to disclose, for any algorithmic decision-support tool integrated into certified EHR systems, specific information: the intended use case, the target population the model was developed and validated on, known performance limitations and risks, characteristics of the training data, and details about the validation process. This isn't theoretical stuff. When a hospital implements an AI-assisted sepsis detection tool or a readmission risk prediction model, clinicians need to know whether that model was trained on populations that look like their patients, what happens when it fails, and what the actual false positive rate is in practice.<br><br>The proposal suggests that vendor discretion and market forces will ensure adequate transparency. I'm skeptical of that, and here's why: health systems don't have the technical capacity to reverse-engineer or independently validate machine learning models. We can evaluate whether a FHIR endpoint works correctly. We can test whether data comes back in the right format. We cannot look at a black-box model and determine whether it's safe to use in our patient population. The asymmetry of information between vendors and health systems is profound. And unlike a CRM system or a billing platform‚Äîplaces where errors are inconvenient and costly‚Äîerrors in clinical decision support can harm patients.<br><br>Over the past eighteen months, I've watched our clinical teams struggle with exactly this problem. We implemented a vendor's length-of-stay prediction model in 2022. It was certified. It looked fine on paper. But six months in, our quality team noticed the model was systematically overestimating length of stay for patients over 75, which skewed resource allocation and created problems for discharge planning. We contacted the vendor. They told us the model was trained on data from three large academic centers with relatively young populations. That information was not disclosed at implementation. It should have been. We spent weeks figuring this out ourselves. A patient didn't die‚Äîthank God‚Äîbut the risk was real, and preventable disclosure would have caught this faster.<br><br>Here's what I think gets lost in the deregulation framing: removing the model card requirement doesn't actually reduce burden on vendors in a meaningful way. It shifts burden to health systems. We end up doing the validation work ourselves, or worse, we don't do it and we deploy models we don't fully understand. That's not innovation. That's risk transfer.<br><br>I also want to separate this from the legitimate deregulation efforts in the proposal that make sense. Streamlining USCDI data class definitions, revising the prescriptive technical standards that lock vendors into specific versions of FHIR or HL7‚Äîthose changes can reduce friction without sacrificing safety. The field has matured enough that we can trust vendors to implement standards competently without micromanaging the technical approach. But AI model transparency isn't the same category. We're not talking about implementation flexibility. We're talking about whether clinicians can make informed decisions about whether to use a tool.<br><br>What would a better approach look like? I'd propose keeping the model card requirement but streamlining it. Instead of demanding a 40-page technical document, require a structured disclosure: intended use, target population characteristics, validation metrics in a standard format, known limitations. Make it machine-readable if possible. Allow vendors to update it as they refine models. This is not burdensome. It's the cost of deploying tools in a regulated healthcare environment. And frankly, vendors who have done solid validation work have nothing to hide‚Äîthey want to showcase it.<br><br>There's also a secondary issue worth mentioning. The proposal mentions clarifying "information blocking" regulations, particularly around vendor-imposed restrictions on data access. I support that effort, genuinely. We recognize the genuine desire to reduce unnecessary burden on health IT developers and support innovation that serves patients and providers. At the same time, we have significant concerns about specific proposals that would weaken data security transparency requirements, particularly around artificial intelligence model disclosures in pharmacy systems and health information exchanges.<br><br>Our organization represents pharmacy networks, data stewards, and patient advocates across the country. We work every day with the real consequences of data breaches and inadequate security practices in healthcare. Last year, I watched what happened to my neighbor when her pharmacy switched to a new system without proper safeguards. Her diabetes information was shared with supplement companies and clinics she'd never heard of. She started getting calls at home. She was terrified, not just about privacy, but about what it meant that no one seemed to be checking whether these new systems were actually safe before they went live. That's what pushed me and our coalition to focus on this issue.<br><br>The specific concern we have is about the proposal to remove or significantly weaken the detailed AI model card disclosure requirement under the Data Security and Integrity (DSI) certification criterion. The current rule requires health IT developers to disclose intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes. This isn't bureaucratic overhead. It's foundational to patient safety.<br><br>Let me explain why this matters in concrete terms. When a pharmacy system uses AI to flag potentially dangerous drug interactions, or when a hospital uses algorithms to route patient information, those decisions affect real people. If the algorithm was trained on data from a particular demographic and doesn't perform well for others, clinicians and pharmacists need to know that. If the system has known limitations with elderly patients taking multiple medications, that's critical information. Right now, the model card requirement ensures that information gets documented and reviewed before deployment. Without it, we're essentially asking healthcare workers to trust algorithms they can't examine.<br><br>I understand the argument that requiring detailed disclosures slows down product development and adds cost. Those are real concerns. But the cost of getting it wrong is also real. A pharmacy system that misidentifies contraindications for an older patient could cause serious harm. A hospital interoperability platform that loses or corrupts patient records creates liability for everyone involved. And from a market perspective, healthcare providers actually want transparency. They're liable for the systems they adopt. Hospitals and pharmacy chains aren't going to implement an AI system if they can't verify it's been properly validated.<br><br>What we're asking is that you retain the current DSI certification requirement for detailed AI model card disclosures. Not as a burden, but as a baseline standard for systems that handle sensitive health information. Here's why this makes sense:<br><br>First, transparency supports clinical adoption. Hospitals and pharmacies will use these systems more confidently if they can review validation data and understand known limitations. That actually accelerates innovation because developers can market their products more effectively.<br><br>Second, it creates accountability. When something goes wrong, regulators, providers, and patients need to understand how the system was built and what it was designed to do. Model cards establish a clear record.<br><br>Third, it protects developers too. If a system fails in the field and there's no documentation of its intended use or validation approach, liability falls on the developer. Clear disclosure actually reduces risk for responsible companies.<br><br>I know there are other deregulatory proposals in this RFI, and some of them probably make sense. We're not against all change. But data security and AI transparency in healthcare systems aren't the right places to cut corners. These aren't legacy burdens from a bygone era of regulation. They're responses to real failures and real vulnerabilities that we're still seeing today.<br><br>Our pharmacy networks process millions of prescriptions a week. We see the gaps in system security, the interoperability problems that cause delays and errors, and the patient impact when data moves without proper safeguards. We also see what happens when new systems are deployed without adequate validation. Removing transparency requirements doesn't make those problems go away. It just means we find out about them after patients are affected instead of before.<br><br>I'd also note that the current DSI certification criterion isn't preventing innovation. New pharmacy systems, hospital platforms, and interoperability solutions are being developed and deployed all the time. Developers are meeting the current standards and bringing products to market. The requirement hasn't stalled the industry. The proposed removal of detailed AI model card disclosures‚Äîthe requirements around intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes‚Äîfeels to me like we're solving the wrong problem.<br><br>Let me explain why I think we need to keep those disclosure requirements, even if they feel burdensome.<br><br>Three months ago, we were working with a health system in Northern California implementing an AI-assisted diagnostic tool. This wasn't some regulatory checkbox they were worried about. They had liability concerns, patient safety concerns, and frankly, they wanted to know if the tool was actually going to work for their patient population‚Äîwhich is predominantly older, with higher rates of comorbidities than the training dataset. Without that model card information, they would have had to do their own validation work anyway, which would have taken longer and cost more. But because we had documented all of that up front, they could make an informed decision in weeks instead of months.<br><br>That's the thing about AI in healthcare. It's not like traditional software where you can discover issues through user feedback and push out patches. When an AI model fails in a clinical setting, you're not just annoyed about a glitchy interface. You're potentially talking about a misdiagnosis. You're talking about a provider who was relying on a tool that performed differently than expected. You're talking about liability, patient harm, and loss of trust in the whole system.<br><br>The vendors who are pushing hardest to remove these requirements‚Äîand I say this as someone in the vendor community‚Äîthey're mostly the ones who can afford to absorb the cost of documentation anyway. They already have the infrastructure. What concerns me is that if we strip away the model card requirements, we're not actually reducing burden equally across the industry. We're creating a situation where smaller vendors either have to do the documentation anyway to be competitive, or they cut corners and produce less transparent products. And then the health systems have to do extra work to figure out whether they can trust what they're deploying.<br><br>I've also seen what happens when transparency breaks down. We had a client last year‚Äînot one of our products, but a competitor's tool‚Äîthat was supposed to work for a specific patient population. The vendor had never actually validated it on that population. The health system found out after deployment when the accuracy numbers didn't match what they'd been told. The vendor claimed the documentation wasn't clear, the health system claimed they'd asked the right questions, everyone lost time and money and trust, and ultimately the tool was pulled.<br><br>The AI model card requirements exist because healthcare is different. It's not like consumer software where you can iterate fast and break things. The stakes are higher. And the clinicians who are going to use these tools‚Äîthey need to know what they're working with. They need to understand the boundaries of what the AI can do, what population it was validated on, what the known risks are. That's not regulatory theater. That's patient safety.<br><br>Now, I'm not saying the current certification process is perfect. There are definitely places where we're creating documentation requirements that don't add value. There are redundancies. There are things we could streamline without compromising safety. I'd like to see us look at whether we're duplicating effort across different certification bodies, or whether we're asking for information in five different formats that could be consolidated. Those conversations are worth having.<br><br>But when it comes to AI transparency‚Äîwhen it comes to knowing what we're putting into clinical practice‚ÄîI think we need to be more careful. I think we need to keep the requirement for detailed model cards. Maybe we can standardize the format to make it easier to produce. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0100‚ôîCMS-2025-0050‚ôîJoseph Allen‚ôîPolimorphic, Inc.‚ôî‚ôî‚ôî‚ôîPolymorphic, Inc. argues that AI model card disclosure requirements should be retained and clarified rather than relaxed, as they serve a critical patient safety function by forcing vendors to systematically validate their algorithms and answer cl...‚ôîPolimorphic, Inc. welcomes the opportunity to comment on the proposed modifications to health IT certification criteria and information blocking regulations. As a mid-sized vendor serving health systems and ambulatory practices across the Mountain West, we have direct experience with the compliance friction that accumulates when regulatory guidance shifts or remains ambiguous. That experience shapes our perspective on what clarity and stability actually mean in practice.<br><br>Over the past eighteen months, we've invested substantially in our current system architecture‚Äîroughly $2.3 million in development, validation, and deployment. When clarifications emerge that reinterpret existing guidance, the real cost isn't just the retraining of our compliance team, though that matters. It's the operational uncertainty. Do we redesign now or wait for further guidance? Do we notify existing customers of potential drift, and if so, how do we characterize something that isn't yet an enforcement action but might be one? These decisions ripple across our organization and affect how quickly we can move to market with new capabilities.<br><br>I mention this not as a complaint but as context for why we believe the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures should be retained and, if anything, clarified further rather than relaxed. The logic here is straightforward and has nothing to do with market protection: transparency in high-stakes healthcare AI is foundational to patient safety and clinical adoption, and the model card requirement is one of the few mechanisms we have to ensure that transparency actually happens.<br><br>Let me be direct about what's happened in our conversations with our customers. We've sold AI-driven clinical decision support tools to three health systems in Nevada and Utah over the past two years. Before deployment, clinicians asked us the same questions repeatedly: What population did you train this on? Do you know where it performs poorly? What happens if the patient demographics don't match your training set? These aren't theoretical concerns. They're how clinicians think about risk. One of our customers‚Äîa rural health system in northern Nevada‚Äîexplicitly told us they wouldn't implement our tool without clarity on performance variation across age groups and comorbidity profiles. That's not regulatory burden speaking. That's clinical judgment.<br><br>The model card criterion, as currently structured, forces us to answer those questions upfront and in writing. It requires us to document intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes. Yes, this takes time. Our team spent six weeks pulling together the first model card for our sepsis risk algorithm. But here's what happened: in that process, we discovered a performance gap in patients over 75 with renal impairment. We wouldn't have found that without being forced to systematically document our validation approach. We fixed it before deployment. The alternative‚Äîreleasing the tool and learning about the gap from a patient safety event‚Äîis categorically worse.<br><br>The concern about burden is real, and I don't think it should be dismissed. We are a smaller company. We don't have a dedicated regulatory affairs division the way Cerner or Epic do. But the burden we're discussing isn't the burden of doing the work‚Äîit's the burden of documenting that we did the work carefully. Those are not the same thing. A vendor should be doing validation work regardless. The model card criterion doesn't create that obligation; it makes the obligation visible.<br><br>What we've seen create genuine friction is inconsistency in how model cards are reviewed and what reviewers consider sufficient evidence. We submitted our first model card in November 2023 and received feedback asking for additional detail on our training data provenance. That was reasonable. But the reviewer's email didn't cite specific criteria from the certification guidance‚Äîit referenced an internal memo that apparently clarified expectations but wasn't widely distributed. Our compliance team spent three weeks trying to figure out whether this new standard applied to all our algorithms or just ones using certain data sources. It turned out to apply to all of them, but we only learned that by calling ONC directly.<br><br>This is where I think the deregulatory impulse, while well-intentioned, misses something important. The problem isn't the model card requirement. The problem is that vendors, especially smaller ones, can't reliably predict what regulators will expect. That uncertainty costs more money than the actual documentation work.<br><br>If the goal is to reduce burden on developers while maintaining patient safety guardrails, the path forward isn't to eliminate model card requirements. It's to standardize them. ONC should publish a detailed model card template with worked examples‚Äînot just a list of required fields but actual examples of how a vendor should document training data composition, how to report performance metrics across subgroups, what level of detail is expected for "known risks." Right now, vendors are essentially reverse-engineering expectations from feedback on submissions.
